<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Life</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Life_(disambiguation)" title="Life (disambiguation)">Life (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For "Life" in the personal sense, see <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">Personal life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Everyday_life" title="Everyday life">Everyday life</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">characteristic that distinguishes physical entities that do have biological processes</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable plainlinks selfreference noprint selfref" role="note">For <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(technical_restrictions)#Forbidden_characters" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (technical restrictions)">technical reasons</a>, "Life #9" redirects here. For the Martina McBride song, see <a href="/wiki/Life_Number_9" title="Life Number 9">Life Number 9</a>.</div>

<p><b>Life</b> is a characteristic that distinguishes <a href="/wiki/Physical_body" title="Physical body">physical entities</a> that have <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological processes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signaling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-sustainability" title="Self-sustainability">self-sustaining</a> processes, from those that do not, either because such functions have ceased (they have <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">died</a>), or because they never had such functions and are classified as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inanimate" title="wikt:inanimate">inanimate</a>. Various forms of life exist, such as <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. The criteria can at times be ambiguous and may or may not define <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viroid" title="Viroid">viroids</a>, or potential <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology#Synthetic_life" title="Synthetic biology">synthetic life</a> as "living". <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">Biology</a> is the science concerned with the study of life.
</p><p>The definition of life is controversial. The current definition is that <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_system_(thermodynamics)" title="Open system (thermodynamics)">open systems</a> that maintain <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, are composed of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, have a <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a>, undergo <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, can <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">grow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapt</a> to their environment, respond to <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolve</a>. However, several other biological definitions have been proposed, and there are some borderline cases of life, such as viruses or viroids. In the past, there have been many attempts to define what is meant by "life" through obsolete concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Odic_force" title="Odic force">odic force</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>, that have now been disproved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_research" title="Biological research">biological discoveries</a>. <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a> describes the natural process of life arising from non-living matter, such as simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>. Properties common to all organisms include the need for certain core <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> to sustain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical" title="Biochemical">biochemical</a> functions.
</p><p>Life on Earth <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">first appeared</a> as early as 4.28 billion years ago, soon after <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#Water_in_the_development_of_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">ocean formation 4.41 billion years ago</a>, and not long after the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formation of the Earth</a> 4.54 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170301_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170301-4">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170301_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170301-5">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20170301_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20170301-6">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.3b_oldest_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-4.3b_oldest-7">[4]</a></sup> Earth's current life may have descended from an <a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a>, although <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>-based life may not have been the first. The mechanism by which <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">life began on Earth</a> is unknown, though many hypotheses have been formulated and are often based on the <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">earliest known life forms</a> are microfossils of bacteria. 3.45 billion year old <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> rocks are reported to have contained <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WU-20171218_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-WU-20171218-8">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-2017_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-2017-9">[6]</a></sup> In 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> thought to be present in the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all living organisms, already a complex organism and not the first living thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160725_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160725-10">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since its primordial beginnings, life on Earth has changed its environment on a <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">geologic time scale</a>. To survive in most <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, life must often adapt to a wide range of conditions. Some microorganisms, called <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a>, thrive in physically or geochemically <a href="/wiki/Extreme_environment" title="Extreme environment">extreme environments</a> that are detrimental to most other life on Earth. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> was the first person to <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">classify</a> organisms. Later, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" title="Linnean taxonomy">his system</a> of <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial nomenclature</a> for the classification of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. Eventually new groups and categories of life were discovered, such as cells and microorganisms, forcing dramatic revisions of the structure of relationships between living organisms. The cell is considered the structural and functional unit of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup> There are two kinds of cells, <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a>, both of which consist of <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> enclosed within a <a href="/wiki/Membrane" title="Membrane">membrane</a> and contain many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomolecules" title="Biomolecules">biomolecules</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>. Cells reproduce through a process of <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>, in which the parent cell divides into two or more daughter cells.
</p><p>Though currently only known on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, life need not be restricted to it, and many scientists speculate in the existence of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>. <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">Artificial life</a> is a computer simulation or man-made reconstruction of any aspect of life, which is often used to examine systems related to natural life. Death is the permanent termination of all <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological functions</a> which sustain an organism, and as such, is the end of its life. <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a> is the process by which an entire group or <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a>, normally a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, dies out. <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">Fossils</a> are the preserved remains or <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">traces</a> of organisms.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<p>The definition of life has long been a challenge for scientists and philosophers, with many varied definitions put forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_2009_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions_2009-12">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmeche1997_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emmeche1997-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-14">[11]</a></sup> This is partially because life is a process, not a substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DefinitionMotivation_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-DefinitionMotivation-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeedingBook_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeedingBook-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> This is complicated by a lack of knowledge of the characteristics of living entities, if any, that may have developed outside of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bioethics_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bioethics-19">[16]</a></sup> Philosophical definitions of life have also been put forward, with similar difficulties on how to distinguish living things from the non-living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeuken1975_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeuken1975-20">[17]</a></sup> Legal definitions of life have also been described and debated, though these generally focus on the decision to declare a human dead, and the legal ramifications of this decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capron1978_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capron1978-21">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">Organism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Characteristics_of_life.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="512" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Characteristics_of_life.svg/220px-Characteristics_of_life.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Characteristics_of_life.svg/330px-Characteristics_of_life.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Characteristics_of_life.svg/440px-Characteristics_of_life.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Characteristics_of_life.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The characteristics of life</div></div></div>
<p>Since there is no unequivocal definition of life, most current definitions in biology are descriptive. Life is considered a characteristic of something that preserves, furthers or reinforces its existence in the given environment. This characteristic exhibits all or most of the following traits:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koshland_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koshland-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBS-2012Feb_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBS-2012Feb-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b><a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">Homeostasis</a></b>: regulation of the internal environment to maintain a constant state; for example, sweating to reduce temperature</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_organization" title="Biological organization">Organization</a></b>: being structurally composed of one or more <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> – the basic units of life</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">Metabolism</a></b>: transformation of energy by converting chemicals and energy into cellular components (<a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>) and decomposing organic matter (<a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a>). Living things require <a href="/wiki/Bioenergetics" title="Bioenergetics">energy</a> to maintain internal organization (homeostasis) and to produce the other phenomena associated with life.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">Growth</a></b>: maintenance of a higher rate of anabolism than catabolism. A growing organism increases in size in all of its parts, rather than simply accumulating matter.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">Adaptation</a></b>: the ability to change over time in response to the environment. This ability is fundamental to the process of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> and is determined by the organism's <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, diet, and external factors.</li>
<li><b>Response to <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a></b>: a response can take many forms, from the contraction of a <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular organism</a> to external chemicals, to complex reactions involving all the senses of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular_organisms" title="Multicellular organisms">multicellular organisms</a>. A response is often expressed by motion; for example, the leaves of a plant turning toward the sun (<a href="/wiki/Phototropism" title="Phototropism">phototropism</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">Reproduction</a></b>: the ability to produce new individual organisms, either <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually</a> from a single parent organism or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexually</a> from two parent organisms.</li></ol>
<p>These complex processes, called <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological functions</a>, have underlying physical and chemical bases, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signalling_(biology)" title="Signalling (biology)">signaling and control mechanisms</a> that are essential to maintaining life.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_definitions">Alternative definitions</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Entropy_and_life" title="Entropy and life">Entropy and life</a></div>
<p>From a <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> perspective, living beings are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_systems" title="Thermodynamic systems">thermodynamic systems</a> with an organized molecular structure that can reproduce itself and evolve as survival dictates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luttermoser-1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luttermoser-1-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luttermoser-2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luttermoser-2-29">[26]</a></sup> Thermodynamically, life has been described as an open system which makes use of gradients in its surroundings to create imperfect copies of itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review_2009_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review_2009-30">[27]</a></sup> Hence, life is a self-sustained chemical system capable of undergoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwinian_evolution" title="Darwinian evolution">Darwinian evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151028_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151028-32">[29]</a></sup> A major strength of this definition is that it distinguishes life by the evolutionary process rather than its chemical composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Domagal_2016_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Domagal_2016-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Others take a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_systems_theory" title="Living systems theory">systemic</a> viewpoint that does not necessarily depend on molecular chemistry. One systemic definition of life is that living things are <a href="/wiki/Self-organization" title="Self-organization">self-organizing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autopoiesis" title="Autopoiesis">autopoietic</a> (self-producing). Variations of this definition include <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Kauffman" title="Stuart Kauffman">Stuart Kauffman</a>'s definition as an <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_agent" title="Autonomous agent">autonomous agent</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Multi-agent_system" title="Multi-agent system">multi-agent system</a> capable of reproducing itself or themselves, and of completing at least one <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_cycle" title="Thermodynamic cycle">thermodynamic work cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> This definition is extended by the apparition of novel functions over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Viruses">Viruses</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">Virus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="499" data-file-width="700" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg/220px-Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg/330px-Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg/440px-Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adenovirus_transmission_electron_micrograph_B82-0142_lores.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenovirus" title="Adenovirus">Adenovirus</a> as seen under an electron microscope</div></div></div>
<p>Whether or not viruses should be considered as alive is controversial. They are most often considered as just <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">replicators</a> rather than forms of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> They have been described as "organisms at the edge of life"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> because they possess <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, evolve by natural selection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17914905_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17914905-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forterre_2010_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forterre_2010-39">[36]</a></sup> and replicate by creating multiple copies of themselves through self-assembly. However, viruses do not metabolize and they require a host cell to make new products. Virus self-assembly within host cells has implications for the study of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">origin of life</a>, as it may support the hypothesis that life could have started as self-assembling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecules" title="Organic molecules">organic molecules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16984643_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16984643-40">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biophysics">Biophysics</span></h3>
<p>To reflect the minimum phenomena required, other biological definitions of life have been proposed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Popa-2004_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Popa-2004-43">[40]</a></sup> with many of these being based upon chemical <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">systems</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">Biophysicists</a> have commented that living things function on <a href="/wiki/Negentropy" title="Negentropy">negative entropy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> In other words, living processes can be viewed as a delay of the spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(chemistry)" title="Dispersion (chemistry)">dispersion</a> of the internal energy of biological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a> towards more potential <a href="/wiki/Microstate_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Microstate (statistical mechanics)">microstates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definitions_2009_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definitions_2009-12">[9]</a></sup> In more detail, according to physicists such as <a href="/wiki/John_Desmond_Bernal" title="John Desmond Bernal">John Bernal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger" title="Erwin Schrödinger">Erwin Schrödinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Wigner" title="Eugene Wigner">Eugene Wigner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Scales_Avery" title="John Scales Avery">John Avery</a>, life is a member of the class of phenomena that are <a href="/wiki/Open_system_(systems_theory)" title="Open system (systems theory)">open</a> or continuous systems able to decrease their internal <a href="/wiki/Entropy_and_life" title="Entropy and life">entropy</a> at the expense of substances or <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_free_energy" title="Thermodynamic free energy">free energy</a> taken in from the environment and subsequently rejected in a degraded form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_systems_theories">Living systems theories</span></h3>
<p>Living systems are open <a href="/wiki/Self-organization" title="Self-organization">self-organizing</a> living things that interact with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>. These systems are maintained by flows of information, <a href="/wiki/Energy_flow_(ecology)" title="Energy flow (ecology)">energy</a>, and matter.
</p><p>Some scientists have proposed in the last few decades that a general living systems theory is required to explain the nature of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> Such a general theory would arise out of the ecological and <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological sciences</a> and attempt to map general principles for how all living systems work. Instead of examining phenomena by attempting to break things down into components, a general living systems theory explores phenomena in terms of dynamic patterns of the relationships of organisms with their environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaia_hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</a></div>
<p>The idea that the Earth is alive is found in philosophy and religion, but the first scientific discussion of it was by the Scottish scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>. In 1785, he stated that the Earth was a superorganism and that its proper study should be <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>. Hutton is considered the father of geology, but his idea of a living Earth was forgotten in the intense <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reductionism</a> of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>10</span></sup> The Gaia hypothesis, proposed in the 1960s by scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovelock1965_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovelock1965-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> suggests that life on Earth functions as a single organism that defines and maintains <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environmental</a> conditions necessary for its survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[47]</a></sup> This hypothesis served as one of the foundations of the modern <a href="/wiki/Earth_system_science" title="Earth system science">Earth system science</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonfractionability">Nonfractionability</span></h4>
<p>The first attempt at a general <a href="/wiki/Living_systems" title="Living systems">living systems</a> theory for explaining the nature of life was in 1978, by American biologist <a href="/wiki/James_Grier_Miller" title="James Grier Miller">James Grier Miller</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rosen_(theoretical_biologist)" title="Robert Rosen (theoretical biologist)">Robert Rosen</a> (1991) built on this by defining a system component as "a unit of organization; a part with a function, i.e., a definite relation between part and whole." From this and other starting concepts, he developed a "relational theory of systems" that attempts to explain the special properties of life. Specifically, he identified the "nonfractionability of components in an organism" as the fundamental difference between living systems and "biological machines."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_as_a_property_of_ecosystems">Life as a property of ecosystems</span></h4>
<p>A systems view of life treats environmental <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">fluxes</a> and biological fluxes together as a "reciprocity of influence,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> and a reciprocal relation with environment is arguably as important for understanding life as it is for understanding ecosystems. As <a href="/wiki/Harold_J._Morowitz" title="Harold J. Morowitz">Harold J. Morowitz</a> (1992) explains it, life is a property of an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecological system</a> rather than a single organism or species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> He argues that an ecosystemic definition of life is preferable to a strictly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical" title="Biochemical">biochemical</a> or physical one. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ulanowicz" title="Robert Ulanowicz">Robert Ulanowicz</a> (2009) highlights mutualism as the key to understand the systemic, order-generating behavior of life and ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_systems_biology">Complex systems biology</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complex_systems_biology" title="Complex systems biology">Complex systems biology</a></div><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_biology" title="Mathematical biology">Mathematical biology</a></div>
<p>Complex systems biology (CSB) is a field of science that studies the emergence of complexity in functional organisms from the viewpoint of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_systems" title="Dynamic systems">dynamic systems</a> theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> The latter is also often called <a href="/wiki/Systems_biology" title="Systems biology">systems biology</a> and aims to understand the most fundamental aspects of life. A closely related approach to CSB and systems biology called relational biology is concerned mainly with understanding life processes in terms of the most important relations, and categories of such relations among the essential functional components of organisms; for multicellular organisms, this has been defined as "categorical biology", or a model representation of organisms as a <a href="/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">category theory</a> of biological relations, as well as an <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Functional_organization" title="Functional organization">functional organization</a> of living organisms in terms of their dynamic, complex <a href="/wiki/Biological_network" title="Biological network">networks</a> of metabolic, genetic, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> processes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signaling_pathway" title="Signaling pathway">signaling pathways</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> Alternative but closely related approaches focus on the interdependance of constraints, where constraints can be either molecular, such as enzymes, or macroscopic, such as the geometry of a bone or of the vascular system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Darwinian_dynamic">Darwinian dynamic</span></h4>
<p>It has also been argued that the evolution of order in living systems and certain physical systems obeys a common fundamental principle termed the Darwinian dynamic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_Bernstein,_Henry_C._Byerly,_Frederick_A._Hopf,_Richard_A._Michod_and_G._Krishna_Vemulapalli_185_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_Bernstein,_Henry_C._Byerly,_Frederick_A._Hopf,_Richard_A._Michod_and_G._Krishna_Vemulapalli_185-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> The Darwinian dynamic was formulated by first considering how macroscopic order is generated in a simple non-biological system far from thermodynamic equilibrium, and then extending consideration to short, replicating <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> molecules. The underlying order-generating process was concluded to be basically similar for both types of systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_Bernstein,_Henry_C._Byerly,_Frederick_A._Hopf,_Richard_A._Michod_and_G._Krishna_Vemulapalli_185_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_Bernstein,_Henry_C._Byerly,_Frederick_A._Hopf,_Richard_A._Michod_and_G._Krishna_Vemulapalli_185-62">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Operator_theory">Operator theory</span></h4>
<p>Another systemic definition called the operator theory proposes that "life is a general term for the presence of the typical closures found in organisms; the typical closures are a membrane and an autocatalytic set in the cell"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> and that an organism is any system with an organisation that complies with an operator type that is at least as complex as the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> Life can also be modeled as a network of inferior <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedbacks</a> of regulatory mechanisms subordinated to a superior <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> formed by the potential of expansion and reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materialism">Materialism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">Materialism</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1072" data-file-width="1424" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg/220px-Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg/330px-Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg/440px-Hoh_rain_forest_trees.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Plant growth in the <a href="/wiki/Hoh_Rainforest" title="Hoh Rainforest">Hoh Rainforest</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herds_Maasi_Mara_(cropped_and_straightened).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="626" data-file-width="966" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg/220px-Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg/330px-Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg/440px-Herds_Maasi_Mara_%28cropped_and_straightened%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Herds of zebra and impala gathering on the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_Mara" title="Maasai Mara">Maasai Mara</a> plain</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1277" data-file-width="1999" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/220px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/330px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg/440px-Grand_prismatic_spring.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An aerial photo of microbial mats around the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prismatic_Spring" title="Grand Prismatic Spring">Grand Prismatic Spring</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Some of the earliest theories of life were materialist, holding that all that exists is matter, and that life is merely a complex form or arrangement of matter. <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> (430 BC) argued that everything in the universe is made up of a combination of <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">four eternal "elements"</a> or "roots of all": earth, water, air, and fire. All change is explained by the arrangement and rearrangement of these four elements. The various forms of life are caused by an appropriate mixture of elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (460 BC) thought that the essential characteristic of life is having a <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> (<i>psyche</i>). Like other ancient writers, he was attempting to explain what makes something a <i>living</i> thing. His explanation was that fiery atoms make a soul in exactly the same way atoms and void account for any other thing. He elaborates on fire because of the apparent connection between life and heat, and because fire moves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-democritus_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-democritus-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Plato's world of eternal and unchanging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">Forms</a>, imperfectly represented in matter by a divine <a href="/wiki/Demiurge" title="Demiurge">Artisan</a>, contrasts sharply with the various mechanistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weltanschauungen" title="Weltanschauungen">Weltanschauungen</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a> was, by the fourth century at least, the most prominent ... This debate persisted throughout the ancient world. Atomistic mechanism got a shot in the arm from <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> ... while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stoics" title="Stoics">Stoics</a> adopted a divine teleology ... The choice seems simple: either show how a structured, regular world could arise out of undirected processes, or inject intelligence into the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>R. J. Hankinson, <i>Cause and Explanation in Ancient Greek Thought</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(philosophy)" title="Mechanism (philosophy)">mechanistic</a> materialism that originated in ancient Greece was revived and revised by the French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, who held that animals and humans were assemblages of parts that together functioned as a machine. In the 19th century, the advances in <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">cell theory</a> in biological science encouraged this view. The <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> theory of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> (1859) is a mechanistic explanation for the origin of species by means of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hylomorphism">Hylomorphism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">Hylomorphism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelian_Soul.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="913" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/330px-Aristotelian_Soul.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/495px-Aristotelian_Soul.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/660px-Aristotelian_Soul.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelian_Soul.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Soul#Aristotle" title="Soul">structure of the souls</a> of plants, animals, and humans, according to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a></div></div></div>
<p>Hylomorphism is a theory first expressed by the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (322 BC). The application of hylomorphism to biology was important to Aristotle, and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">biology is extensively covered in his extant writings</a>. In this view, everything in the material universe has both matter and form, and the form of a living thing is its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a> (Greek <i>psyche</i>, Latin <i>anima</i>). There are three kinds of souls: the <i>vegetative soul</i> of plants, which causes them to grow and decay and nourish themselves, but does not cause motion and sensation; the <i>animal soul</i>, which causes animals to move and feel; and the <i>rational soul</i>, which is the source of consciousness and reasoning, which (Aristotle believed) is found only in man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> Each higher soul has all of the attributes of the lower ones. Aristotle believed that while matter can exist without form, form cannot exist without matter, and that therefore the soul cannot exist without the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>This account is consistent with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleological" title="Teleological">teleological</a> explanations of life, which account for phenomena in terms of purpose or goal-directedness. Thus, the whiteness of the polar bear's coat is explained by its purpose of camouflage. The direction of causality (from the future to the past) is in contradiction with the scientific evidence for natural selection, which explains the consequence in terms of a prior cause. Biological features are explained not by looking at future optimal results, but by looking at the past <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history" title="Evolutionary history">evolutionary history</a> of a species, which led to the natural selection of the features in question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stewert_williams2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-stewert_williams2010-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spontaneous_generation">Spontaneous generation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">Spontaneous generation</a></div>
<p>Spontaneous generation was the belief that living organisms can form without descent from similar organisms. Typically, the idea was that certain forms such as fleas could arise from inanimate matter such as dust or the supposed seasonal generation of mice and insects from mud or garbage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory of spontaneous generation was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> who compiled and expanded the work of prior natural philosophers and the various ancient explanations of the appearance of organisms; it held sway for two millennia. It was decisively dispelled by the experiments of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> in 1859, who expanded upon the investigations of predecessors such as <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Redi" title="Francesco Redi">Francesco Redi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> Disproof of the traditional ideas of spontaneous generation is no longer controversial among biologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernal_1967_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernal_1967-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szathmary_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szathmary-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitalism">Vitalism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">Vitalism</a></div>
<p>Vitalism is the belief that the life-principle is non-material. This originated with <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ernst_Stahl" title="Georg Ernst Stahl">Georg Ernst Stahl</a> (17th century), and remained popular until the middle of the 19th century. It appealed to philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Dilthey" title="Wilhelm Dilthey">Wilhelm Dilthey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> anatomists like <a href="/wiki/Marie_Fran%C3%A7ois_Xavier_Bichat" title="Marie François Xavier Bichat">Marie François Xavier Bichat</a>, and chemists like <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson-85">[82]</a></sup> Vitalism included the idea that there was a fundamental difference between organic and inorganic material, and the belief that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic material</a> can only be derived from living things. This was disproved in 1828, when <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> prepared <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> from inorganic materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/W%C3%B6hler_synthesis" title="Wöhler synthesis">Wöhler synthesis</a> is considered the starting point of modern <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. It is of historical significance because for the first time an <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compound</a> was produced in <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic</a> reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1850s, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a>, anticipated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius_Robert_von_Mayer" title="Julius Robert von Mayer">Julius Robert von Mayer</a>, demonstrated that no energy is lost in muscle movement, suggesting that there were no "vital forces" necessary to move a muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> These results led to the abandonment of scientific interest in vitalistic theories, although the belief lingered on in <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a> theories such as <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, which interprets diseases and sickness as caused by disturbances in a hypothetical vital force or life force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h2>
