width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: 002px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:14px 4px 14px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Huronian_glaciation" title="Huronian glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Huronian</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:5.3722466960352em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1.15em; left:-13.5em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: -003px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 9px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:3px 4px 3px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian"><span style="color:black;"><small>Cryogenian</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:3.4493392070485em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1.15em; left:-13.7em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: 009px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Andean-Saharan_glaciation" title="Andean-Saharan glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Andean</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:2.4581497797357em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1em; left:-13.8em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: 015px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Karoo_Ice_Age" title="Karoo Ice Age"><span style="color:black;"><small>Karoo</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:0.010308370044053em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.28em; left:-13.55em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: 000px; top: -001px"><span style="font:bold 9px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Quaternary</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:0em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1.5em; left:-13.5em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="font:bold 9px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><b>Ice_Ages</b></span></a></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div></div></div><div class="toccolours" id="Legend" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div><div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><div style="float: right;"><span style="font-size:85%;">(<a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">view</a> • <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Life_timeline" title="Template talk:Life timeline">discuss</a>)</span></div><div style="float: left;"><small><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">million years</a></small></div><br/><span style="float:right;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="133" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18"/></span><div style="float: left;"><small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a> is about 4.54 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1997_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> Evidence suggests that life on Earth has existed for at least 3.5 <a href="/wiki/Bya" title="Bya">billion years</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AB-20021014_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-AB-20021014-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> with the oldest physical <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">traces</a> of life dating back 3.7 billion years;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-102">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-103">[100]</a></sup> however, some theories, such as the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment#Geological_consequences_on_Earth" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment theory</a>, suggest that life on Earth may have started even earlier, as early as 4.1–4.4 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AB-20021014_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-AB-20021014-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-96">[93]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">chemistry leading to life</a> may have begun shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">13.8 billion years ago</a>, during an epoch when the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> was only 10–17 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJA-2014October_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJA-2014October-104">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARXIV-20131202_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARXIV-20131202-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141202_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141202-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than 99% of all species of life forms, amounting to over five billion species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-107">[104]</a></sup> that ever lived on Earth are estimated to be <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the number of Earth's catalogued species of lifeforms is between 1.2 million and 2 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLoS-20110823_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-20110823-111">[108]</a></sup> the total number of species in the planet is uncertain. Estimates range from 8 million to 100 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLoS-20110823_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-20110823-111">[108]</a></sup> with a more narrow range between 10 and 14 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-110">[107]</a></sup> but it may be as high as 1 trillion (with only one-thousandth of one percent of the species described) according to studies realized in May 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LiveScience_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-LiveScience-113">[110]</a></sup> The total amount of related <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> on Earth is estimated at 5.0 x 10<sup>37</sup> and weighs 50 billion tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150718-rn_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150718-rn-114">[111]</a></sup> In comparison, the total mass of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> has been estimated to be as much as 4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes#Derived_units" title="Tonnes">TtC</a> (trillion tons of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGCI-2015_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGCI-2015-115">[112]</a></sup> In July 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Universal_Common_Ancestor" title="Last Universal Common Ancestor">Last Universal Common Ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all organisms living on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160725_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160725-10">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>All known life forms share fundamental molecular mechanisms, reflecting their <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common descent</a>; based on these observations, hypotheses on the origin of life attempt to find a mechanism explaining the formation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_common_ancestor" title="Universal common ancestor">universal common ancestor</a>, from simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> via pre-cellular life to <a href="/wiki/Protocell" title="Protocell">protocells</a> and metabolism. Models have been divided into "genes-first" and "metabolism-first" categories, but a recent trend is the emergence of hybrid models that combine both categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no current <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> as to how life originated. However, most accepted scientific models build on the <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a> and the work of <a href="/wiki/Sidney_W._Fox" title="Sidney W. Fox">Sidney Fox</a>, which show that conditions on the primitive Earth favored chemical reactions that synthesize <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> and other organic compounds from inorganic precursors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> spontaneously form <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayers</a>, the basic structure of a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>.
</p><p>Living organisms synthesize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> of amino acids using instructions encoded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" title="Protein synthesis">Protein synthesis</a> entails intermediary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribonucleic_acid" title="Ribonucleic acid">ribonucleic acid</a> (RNA) polymers. One possibility for how life began is that genes originated first, followed by proteins;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> the alternative being that proteins came first and then genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, because genes and proteins are both required to produce the other, the problem of considering which came first is like that of the <a href="/wiki/Chicken_or_the_egg" title="Chicken or the egg">chicken or the egg</a>. Most scientists have adopted the hypothesis that because of this, it is unlikely that genes and proteins arose independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Therefore, a possibility, first suggested by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> is that the first life was based on <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-120">[117]</a></sup> which has the DNA-like properties of information storage and the <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalytic</a> properties of some proteins. This is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_world_hypothesis" title="RNA world hypothesis">RNA world hypothesis</a>, and it is supported by the observation that many of the most critical components of cells (those that <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolve</a> the slowest) are composed mostly or entirely of RNA. Also, many critical cofactors (<a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">Acetyl-CoA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADH" title="NADH">NADH</a>, etc.) are either nucleotides or substances clearly related to them. The catalytic properties of RNA had not yet been demonstrated when the hypothesis was first proposed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> but they were confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cech" title="Thomas Cech">Thomas Cech</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>One issue with the RNA world hypothesis is that synthesis of RNA from simple inorganic precursors is more difficult than for other organic molecules. One reason for this is that RNA precursors are very stable and react with each other very slowly under ambient conditions, and it has also been proposed that living organisms consisted of other molecules before RNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cech_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cech-124">[121]</a></sup> However, the successful synthesis of certain RNA molecules under the conditions that existed prior to life on Earth has been achieved by adding alternative precursors in a specified order with the precursor <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> present throughout the reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> This study makes the RNA world hypothesis more plausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Geological findings in 2013 showed that reactive <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> species (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphite" title="Phosphite">phosphite</a>) were in abundance in the ocean before 3.5 Ga, and that <a href="/wiki/Schreibersite" title="Schreibersite">Schreibersite</a> easily reacts with aqueous <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> to generate phosphite and <a href="/wiki/Glycerol_3-phosphate" title="Glycerol 3-phosphate">glycerol 3-phosphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasek_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pasek-127">[124]</a></sup> It is hypothesized that <a href="/wiki/Schreibersite" title="Schreibersite">Schreibersite</a>-containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">meteorites</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> could have provided early reduced phosphorus, which could react with prebiotic organic molecules to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphorylate" title="Phosphorylate">phosphorylated</a> biomolecules, like <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasek_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pasek-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, experiments demonstrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwinian_evolution" title="Darwinian evolution">Darwinian evolution</a> of a two-component system of RNA enzymes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribozymes" title="Ribozymes">ribozymes</a>) <i>in vitro</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> The work was performed in the laboratory of <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Joyce" title="Gerald Joyce">Gerald Joyce</a>, who stated "This is the first example, outside of biology, of evolutionary adaptation in a molecular genetic system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prebiotic compounds may have originated extraterrestrially. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> findings in 2011, based on studies with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">meteorites</a> found on Earth, suggest <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and RNA components (<a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> and related organic molecules) may be formed in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callahan_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callahan-130">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steigerwald_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steigerwald-131">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-132">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lincei_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lincei-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2015, NASA scientists reported that, for the first time, complex <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> of life, including <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>, have been formed in the laboratory under <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> conditions, using starting chemicals, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. Pyrimidine, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs), the most <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>-rich chemical found in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, may have been formed in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a> and gas clouds, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150303_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150303-134">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Panspermia" title="Panspermia">panspermia</a> hypothesis, <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microscopic life</a>—distributed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteoroids" title="Meteoroids">meteoroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>—may exist throughout the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USRA-2010_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-USRA-2010-135">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_conditions">Environmental conditions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2222" data-file-width="2222" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg/220px-20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg/330px-20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg/440px-20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20100422_235222_Cyanobacteria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_catastrophe" title="Oxygen catastrophe">dramatically changed</a> the composition of life forms on Earth by leading to the near-extinction of <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">oxygen-intolerant organisms</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The diversity of life on Earth is a result of the dynamic interplay between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_opportunity" title="Genetic opportunity">genetic opportunity</a>, metabolic capability, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environmental</a> challenges,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-136">[133]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup> For most of its existence, Earth's habitable environment has been dominated by <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> and subjected to their metabolism and evolution. As a consequence of these microbial activities, the physical-chemical environment on Earth has been changing on a <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">geologic time scale</a>, thereby affecting the path of evolution of subsequent life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-136">[133]</a></sup> For example, the release of molecular <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> induced global changes in the Earth's environment. Because oxygen was toxic to most life on Earth at the time, this posed novel evolutionary challenges, and ultimately resulted in the formation of Earth's major animal and plant species. This interplay between organisms and their environment is an inherent feature of living systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosphere">Biosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">Biosphere</a></div>
<p>The biosphere is the global sum of all ecosystems. It can also be termed as the zone of life on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, a closed system (apart from solar and cosmic radiation and heat from the interior of the Earth), and largely self-regulating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncyc_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncyc-140">[137]</a></sup> By the most general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geophysiology" title="Geophysiology">biophysiological</a> definition, the biosphere is the global ecological system integrating all living beings and their relationships, including their interaction with the elements of the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geosphere" title="Geosphere">geosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.
</p><p>Life forms live in every part of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endolith" title="Endolith">inside rocks</a> at least 19 km (12 mi) deep underground, the deepest parts of the ocean, and at least 64 km (40 mi) high in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD-19980625-UG_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-SD-19980625-UG-141">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABM-20150112_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABM-20150112-142">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20151124_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151124-143">[140]</a></sup> Under certain test conditions, life forms have been observed to thrive in the <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness#Effects_on_non-human_organisms" title="Weightlessness">near-weightlessness</a> of space<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZM-20170913_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZM-20170913-144">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASU-20070923_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASU-20070923-145">[142]</a></sup> and to <a href="/wiki/Panspermia#Research_in_outer_space" title="Panspermia">survive in the vacuum of outer space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dose_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dose-146">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horneck_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horneck-147">[144]</a></sup> Life forms appear to thrive in the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a>, the deepest spot in the Earth's oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20130317_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20130317-149">[146]</a></sup> Other researchers reported related studies that life forms thrive inside rocks up to 580 m (1,900 ft; 0.36 mi) below the sea floor under 2,590 m (8,500 ft; 1.61 mi) of ocean off the coast of the northwestern United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130314_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130314-150">[147]</a></sup> as well as 2,400 m (7,900 ft; 1.5 mi) beneath the seabed off Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20141215-RM_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20141215-RM-151">[148]</a></sup> In August 2014, scientists confirmed the existence of life forms living 800 m (2,600 ft; 0.50 mi) below the ice of Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140820_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140820-152">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB-20140820_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRB-20140820-153">[150]</a></sup> According to one researcher, "You can find <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a> everywhere — they're extremely adaptable to conditions, and survive wherever they are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-148">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biosphere is postulated to have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a>, beginning with a process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">biopoesis</a> (life created naturally from non-living matter, such as simple organic compounds) or <a href="/wiki/Biogenesis" title="Biogenesis">biogenesis</a> (life created from living matter), at least some 3.5 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_2006_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell_2006-154">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-155">[152]</a></sup> The earliest evidence for life on Earth includes <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> found in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">metasedimentary rocks</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-101">[98]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> from <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-102">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-103">[100]</a></sup> More recently, in 2015, "remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic life</a>" were found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-94">[91]</a></sup> In 2017, putative fossilized <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Micropaleontology#Microfossils" title="Micropaleontology">microfossils</a>) were announced to have been discovered in <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent precipitates</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nuvvuagittuq_Greenstone_Belt" title="Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt">Nuvvuagittuq Belt</a> of Quebec, Canada that were as old as 4.28 billion years, the oldest record of life on earth, suggesting "an almost instantaneous emergence of life" after <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#Water_in_the_development_of_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">ocean formation 4.4 billion years ago</a>, and not long after the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formation of the Earth</a> 4.54 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170301_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170301-4">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170301_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170301-5">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20170301_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20170301-6">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.3b_oldest_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-4.3b_oldest-7">[4]</a></sup> According to biologist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Blair_Hedges" title="Stephen Blair Hedges">Stephen Blair Hedges</a>, "If life arose relatively quickly on Earth ... then it could be common in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-93">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a general sense, biospheres are any closed, self-regulating systems containing ecosystems. This includes artificial biospheres such as <a href="/wiki/Biosphere_2" title="Biosphere 2">Biosphere 2</a> and <a href="/wiki/BIOS-3" title="BIOS-3">BIOS-3</a>, and potentially ones on other planets or moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webdictionary.co.uk_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-webdictionary.co.uk-156">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_of_tolerance">Range of tolerance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="532" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg/150px-Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg/225px-Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg/300px-Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deinococcus_radiodurans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Deinococcus_radiodurans" title="Deinococcus radiodurans">Deinococcus radiodurans</a></i> is an <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> that can resist extremes of cold, dehydration, vacuum, acid, and radiation exposure.</div></div></div>
<p>The inert components of an ecosystem are the physical and chemical factors necessary for life—energy (sunlight or <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">chemical energy</a>), water, heat, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_biology" title="Gravitational biology">gravity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">solar radiation protection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> In most ecosystems, the conditions vary during the day and from one season to the next. To live in most ecosystems, then, organisms must be able to survive a range of conditions, called the "range of tolerance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolerance_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-tolerance-158">[155]</a></sup> Outside that are the "zones of physiological stress," where the survival and reproduction are possible but not optimal. Beyond these zones are the "zones of intolerance," where survival and reproduction of that organism is unlikely or impossible. Organisms that have a wide range of tolerance are more widely distributed than organisms with a narrow range of tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolerance_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-tolerance-158">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremophiles">Extremophiles</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">Extremophile</a></div>
<p>To survive, selected microorganisms can assume forms that enable them to withstand <a href="/wiki/Psychrophile" title="Psychrophile">freezing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xerophile" title="Xerophile">complete desiccation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oligotroph" title="Oligotroph">starvation</a>, high levels of <a href="/wiki/Radioresistance" title="Radioresistance">radiation exposure</a>, and other physical or chemical challenges. These microorganisms may survive exposure to such conditions for weeks, months, years, or even centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_136-3"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-136">[133]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremophiles" title="Extremophiles">Extremophiles</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbial life forms</a> that thrive outside the ranges where life is commonly found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160912_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160912-159">[156]</a></sup> They excel at exploiting uncommon sources of energy. While all organisms are composed of nearly identical <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, evolution has enabled such microbes to cope with this wide range of physical and chemical conditions. Characterization of the <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">structure</a> and metabolic diversity of microbial communities in such <a href="/wiki/Extreme_environment" title="Extreme environment">extreme environments</a> is ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">Microbial life forms</a> thrive even in the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a>, the deepest spot in the Earth's oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_148-3"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20130317_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20130317-149">[146]</a></sup> Microbes also thrive inside <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> up to 1,900 feet (580 m) below the sea floor under 8,500 feet (2,600 m) of ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130317_148-4"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130317-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20130314_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20130314-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Investigation of the tenacity and versatility of life on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160912_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160912-159">[156]</a></sup> as well as an understanding of the molecular systems that some organisms utilize to survive such extremes, is important for the search for <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">life beyond Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobiology_136-4"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-136">[133]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a> could survive for a month in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_on_Earth_under_Martian_conditions" title="Life on Earth under Martian conditions">simulated Martian environment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skymania-20120426_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skymania-20120426-161">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGU-20120426_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-EGU-20120426-162">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_elements">Chemical elements</span></h3>
<p>All life forms require certain core <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> needed for <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> functioning. These include <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>—the elemental <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">macronutrients</a> for all organisms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj20101203_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20101203-163">[160]</a></sup>—often represented by the acronym <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CHNOPS" title="CHNOPS">CHNOPS</a>. Together these make up <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, proteins and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, the bulk of living matter. Five of these six elements comprise the chemical components of DNA, the exception being sulfur. The latter is a component of the amino acids <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>. The most biologically abundant of these elements is carbon, which has the desirable attribute of forming multiple, stable <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. This allows carbon-based (organic) molecules to form an immense variety of chemical arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neuhauss2005_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-neuhauss2005-164">[161]</a></sup> Alternative <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry" title="Hypothetical types of biochemistry">hypothetical types of biochemistry</a> have been proposed that eliminate one or more of these elements, swap out an element for one not on the list, or change required <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">chiralities</a> or other chemical properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA">DNA</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a></div>
<p>Deoxyribonucleic acid is a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> that carries most of the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> instructions used in the growth, development, functioning and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a> of all known living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> and many viruses. DNA and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> are <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a>s; alongside <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">complex carbohydrates</a>, they are one of the three major types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macromolecules" title="Macromolecules">macromolecule</a> that are essential for all known forms of life. Most DNA molecules consist of two <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">biopolymer</a> strands coiled around each other to form a <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_double_helix" title="Nucleic acid double helix">double helix</a>. The two DNA strands are known as <a href="/wiki/Polynucleotide" title="Polynucleotide">polynucleotides</a> since they are composed of <a href="/wiki/Monomer" title="Monomer">simpler units</a> called <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup> Each nucleotide is composed of a <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_base" title="Nitrogenous base">nitrogen-containing</a> <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobase</a>—either <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> (C), <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> (G), <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> (A), or <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a> (T)—as well as a <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">sugar</a> called <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphate_group" title="Phosphate group">phosphate group</a>. The nucleotides are joined to one another in a chain by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a> between the sugar of one nucleotide and the phosphate of the next, resulting in an alternating <a href="/wiki/Backbone_chain" title="Backbone chain">sugar-phosphate backbone</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairing</a> rules (A with T, and C with G), <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> bind the nitrogenous bases of the two separate polynucleotide strands to make double-stranded DNA. The total amount of related DNA <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> on Earth is estimated at 5.0 x 10<sup>37</sup>, and weighs 50 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150718-rn_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150718-rn-114">[111]</a></sup> In comparison, the total <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">mass</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> has been estimated to be as much as 4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes#Derived_units" title="Tonnes">TtC</a> (trillion tons of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGCI-2015_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-AGCI-2015-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>DNA stores biological information. The DNA backbone is resistant to cleavage, and both strands of the double-stranded structure store the same biological information. Biological information is replicated as the two strands are separated. A significant portion of DNA (more than 98% for humans) is <a href="/wiki/Non-coding_DNA" title="Non-coding DNA">non-coding</a>, meaning that these sections do not serve as patterns for protein sequences.
</p><p>The two strands of DNA run in opposite directions to each other and are therefore <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(biochemistry)" title="Antiparallel (biochemistry)">anti-parallel</a>. Attached to each sugar is one of four types of nucleobases (informally, <i>bases</i>). It is the <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">sequence</a> of these four nucleobases along the backbone that encodes biological information. Under the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>, <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> strands are translated to specify the sequence of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> within proteins. These RNA strands are initially created using DNA strands as a template in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a>.
</p><p>Within cells, DNA is organized into long structures called <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>. During <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> these chromosomes are duplicated in the process of <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, providing each cell its own complete set of chromosomes. <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eukaryotic organisms</a> (animals, plants, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>) store most of their DNA inside the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a> and some of their DNA in <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> (bacteria and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>) store their DNA only in the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>. Within the chromosomes, <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">chromatin</a> proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histones</a> compact and organize DNA. These compact structures guide the interactions between DNA and other proteins, helping control which parts of the DNA are transcribed.
</p><p>DNA was first isolated by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Miescher" title="Friedrich Miescher">Friedrich Miescher</a> in 1869.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup> Its molecular structure was identified by <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> in 1953, whose model-building efforts were guided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> data acquired by <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24840850_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24840850-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">Biological classification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg" title="File:Biological classification L Pengo vflip.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="230" height="385" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/150px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/225px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/300px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_131489565" width="150"/></a><map name="ImageMap_1_131489565"><area alt="Life" coords="65,10,147,37" href="/wiki/Life" shape="rect" title="Life"/><area alt="Domain" coords="65,51,147,78" href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Domain"/><area alt="Kingdom" coords="65,93,147,120" href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Kingdom"/><area alt="Phylum" coords="65,134,147,161" href="/wiki/Phylum" shape="rect" title="Phylum"/><area alt="Class" coords="65,175,147,202" href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Class"/><area alt="Order" coords="65,217,147,244" href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Order"/><area alt="Family" coords="65,258,147,285" href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Family"/><area alt="Genus" coords="65,299,147,327" href="/wiki/Genus" shape="rect" title="Genus"/><area alt="Species" coords="65,341,147,368" href="/wiki/Species" shape="rect" title="Species"/></map></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The hierarchy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a>'s eight major <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic ranks</a>. <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Life</a> is divided into domains, which are subdivided into further groups. Intermediate minor rankings are not shown.</div></div></div>
<p>Life is usually classified by eight levels of taxa—domains, kingdoms, phyla, class, order, family, genus, and species. In May 2016, scientists reported that 1 trillion <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> are estimated to be on Earth currently with only one-thousandth of one percent described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first known attempt to classify organisms was conducted by the Greek philosopher Aristotle (384–322 BC), who classified all living organisms known at that time as either a plant or an animal, based mainly on their ability to move. He also distinguished animals with blood from animals without blood (or at least without red blood), which can be compared with the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> respectively, and divided the blooded animals into five groups: viviparous quadrupeds (<a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>), oviparous quadrupeds (reptiles and <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>), birds, fishes and <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">whales</a>. The bloodless animals were also divided into five groups: <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, insects (which included the spiders, <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>, in addition to what we define as insects today), shelled animals (such as most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>), and "<a href="/wiki/Zoophyte" title="Zoophyte">zoophytes</a>" (animals that resemble plants). Though Aristotle's work in zoology was not without errors, it was the grandest biological synthesis of the time and remained the ultimate authority for many centuries after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exploration of the Americas revealed large numbers of new plants and animals that needed descriptions and classification. In the latter part of the 16th century and the beginning of the 17th, careful study of animals commenced and was gradually extended until it formed a sufficient body of knowledge to serve as an anatomical basis for classification. In the late 1740s, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> introduced his system of <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial nomenclature</a> for the classification of species. Linnaeus attempted to improve the composition and reduce the length of the previously used many-worded names by abolishing unnecessary rhetoric, introducing new descriptive terms and precisely defining their meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fungi were originally treated as plants. For a short period Linnaeus had classified them in the taxon <a href="/wiki/Vermes" title="Vermes">Vermes</a> in Animalia, but later placed them back in Plantae. <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Copeland" title="Herbert Copeland">Copeland</a> classified the Fungi in his Protoctista, thus partially avoiding the problem but acknowledging their special status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> The problem was eventually solved by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Whittaker" title="Robert Whittaker">Whittaker</a>, when he gave them their own <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdom</a> in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five-kingdom_system" title="Five-kingdom system">five-kingdom system</a>. <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history</a> shows that the fungi are more closely related to animals than to plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>As new discoveries enabled detailed study of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> and microorganisms, new groups of life were revealed, and the fields of <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cell biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a> were created. These new organisms were originally described separately in <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> as animals and <a href="/wiki/Thallophyte" title="Thallophyte">protophyta/thallophyta</a> as plants, but were united by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Haeckel</a> in the kingdom <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protista" title="Protista">Protista</a>; later, the <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> were split off in the kingdom <a href="/wiki/Monera" title="Monera">Monera</a>, which would eventually be divided into two separate groups, the Bacteria and the <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>. This led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Six-kingdom_system" title="Six-kingdom system">six-kingdom system</a> and eventually to the current <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three-domain system</a>, which is based on evolutionary relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woese1990_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woese1990-175">[172]</a></sup> However, the classification of eukaryotes, especially of protists, is still controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adl_05_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adl_05-176">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>As microbiology, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virology" title="Virology">virology</a> developed, non-cellular reproducing agents were discovered, such as viruses and <a href="/wiki/Viroid" title="Viroid">viroids</a>. Whether these are considered alive has been a matter of debate; viruses lack characteristics of life such as cell membranes, metabolism and the ability to grow or respond to their environments. Viruses can still be classed into "species" based on their biology and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, but many aspects of such a classification remain controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s a trend called <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistics</a> emerged, arranging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">taxa</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">evolutionary or phylogenetic tree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr>
