<h3><span id="Biota_.28taxonomy.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Biota_(taxonomy)">Biota (taxonomy)</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Life&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In systems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">scientific classification</a>, <b>Biota</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn2000_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn2000-186">[183]</a></sup> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superdomain" title="Superdomain">superdomain</a> that classifies all life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taxonomicon_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taxonomicon-187">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">Cell (biology)</a></div>
<p>Cells are the basic unit of structure in every living thing, and all cells arise from pre-existing cells by <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">division</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">Cell theory</a> was formulated by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Dutrochet" title="Henri Dutrochet">Henri Dutrochet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Virchow" title="Rudolf Virchow">Rudolf Virchow</a> and others during the early nineteenth century, and subsequently became widely accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sapp2003_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-sapp2003-189">[186]</a></sup> The activity of an organism depends on the total activity of its cells, with <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">energy flow</a> occurring within and between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-190">[187]</a></sup> Cells contain hereditary information that is carried forward as a <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> code during cell division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two primary types of cells. <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryotes</a> lack a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> and other membrane-bound <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>, although they have circular DNA and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>. Bacteria and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> are two <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domains</a> of prokaryotes. The other primary type of cells are the <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, which have distinct nuclei bound by a nuclear membrane and membrane-bound organelles, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lysosomes" title="Lysosomes">lysosomes</a>, rough and smooth <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacuoles" title="Vacuoles">vacuoles</a>. In addition, they possess organized chromosomes that store genetic material. All species of large complex organisms are eukaryotes, including animals, plants and fungi, though most species of eukaryote are <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protist</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> The conventional model is that eukaryotes evolved from prokaryotes, with the main organelles of the eukaryotes forming through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiosis" title="Endosymbiosis">endosymbiosis</a> between bacteria and the progenitor eukaryotic cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>The molecular mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cell biology</a> are based on <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. Most of these are synthesized by the ribosomes through an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_catalysis" title="Enzyme catalysis">enzyme-catalyzed</a> process called <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein biosynthesis</a>. A sequence of amino acids is assembled and joined together based upon <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> of the cell's nucleic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup> In eukaryotic cells, these proteins may then be transported and processed through the <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a> in preparation for dispatch to their destination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20605430_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20605430-195">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cells reproduce through a process of <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> in which the parent cell divides into two or more daughter cells. For prokaryotes, cell division occurs through a process of <a href="/wiki/Fission_(biology)" title="Fission (biology)">fission</a> in which the DNA is replicated, then the two copies are attached to parts of the cell membrane. In <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, a more complex process of <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> is followed. However, the end result is the same; the resulting cell copies are identical to each other and to the original cell (except for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutations" title="Mutations">mutations</a>), and both are capable of further division following an <a href="/wiki/Interphase" title="Interphase">interphase</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">Multicellular organisms</a> may have first evolved through the formation of <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> of identical cells. These cells can form group organisms through <a href="/wiki/Cell_adhesion" title="Cell adhesion">cell adhesion</a>. The individual members of a colony are capable of surviving on their own, whereas the members of a true multi-cellular organism have developed specializations, making them dependent on the remainder of the organism for survival. Such organisms are formed <a href="/wiki/Clone_(cell_biology)" title="Clone (cell biology)">clonally</a> or from a single <a href="/wiki/Germ_cell" title="Germ cell">germ cell</a> that is capable of forming the various specialized cells that form the adult organism. This specialization allows multicellular organisms to exploit resources more efficiently than single cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[194]</a></sup> In January 2016, scientists reported that, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Timeline of the evolutionary history of life">about 800 million years ago</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology" title="Evolutionary developmental biology">minor genetic change</a> in a single <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a>, called GK-PID, may have allowed <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> to go from a <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">single cell organism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">one of many cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160107_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160107-198">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cells have evolved methods to perceive and respond to their microenvironment, thereby enhancing their adaptability. <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">Cell signaling</a> coordinates cellular activities, and hence governs the basic functions of multicellular organisms. Signaling between cells can occur through direct cell contact using <a href="/wiki/Juxtacrine_signalling" title="Juxtacrine signalling">juxtacrine signalling</a>, or indirectly through the exchange of agents as in the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine system</a>. In more complex organisms, coordination of activities can occur through a dedicated <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alberts2002_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-alberts2002-199">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial">Extraterrestrial</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">Extraterrestrial life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">Astrobiology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astroecology" title="Astroecology">Astroecology</a></div>
<p>Though life is confirmed only on Earth, many think that <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> is not only plausible, but probable or inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RaceRandolph2002_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-RaceRandolph2002-200">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup> Other planets and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moons" title="Moons">moons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a> are being examined for evidence of having once supported simple life, and projects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SETI" title="SETI">SETI</a> are trying to detect radio transmissions from possible alien civilizations. Other locations within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> that may host <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial</a> life include the subsurface of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars_(planet)" title="Life on Mars (planet)">Mars</a>, the upper atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Venus" title="Life on Venus">Venus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[199]</a></sup> and subsurface oceans on some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_satellite_habitability" title="Natural satellite habitability">moons</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRD-20150127_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-WRD-20150127-203">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup>
Beyond the Solar System, the region around another <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence star</a> that could support Earth-like life on an Earth-like planet is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>. The inner and outer radii of this zone vary with the luminosity of the star, as does the time interval during which the zone survives. Stars more massive than the Sun have a larger habitable zone, but remain on the Sun-like "main sequence" of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">stellar evolution</a> for a shorter time interval. Small <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a> have the opposite problem, with a smaller habitable zone that is subject to higher levels of magnetic activity and the effects of <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidal locking</a> from close orbits. Hence, stars in the intermediate mass range such as the Sun may have a greater likelihood for Earth-like life to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selis2006_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-selis2006-205">[202]</a></sup> The location of the star within a galaxy may also affect the likelihood of life forming. Stars in regions with a greater abundance of heavier elements that can form planets, in combination with a low rate of potentially <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a>-damaging <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> events, are predicted to have a higher probability of hosting planets with complex life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science303_5654_59_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-science303_5654_59-206">[203]</a></sup> The variables of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_equation" title="Drake equation">Drake equation</a> are used to discuss the conditions in planetary systems where civilization is most likely to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vakoch_harrison2011_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-vakoch_harrison2011-207">[204]</a></sup> Use of the equation to predict the amount of extraterrestrial life, however, is difficult; because many of the variables are unknown, the equation functions as more of a mirror to what its user already thinks. As a result, the number of civilizations in the galaxy can be estimated as low as 9.1 x 10<sup>−11</sup> or as high as 156 million; for the calculations, see <a href="/wiki/Drake_equation#Range_of_results" title="Drake equation">Drake equation</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial">Artificial</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">Artificial life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">Synthetic biology</a></div>
<p>Artificial life is the <a href="/wiki/Simulation" title="Simulation">simulation</a> of any aspect of life, as through computers, <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup> The study of artificial life imitates traditional biology by recreating some aspects of biological phenomena. Scientists study the logic of living systems by creating artificial environments—seeking to understand the complex information processing that defines such systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-190">[187]</a></sup> While life is, by definition, alive, artificial life is generally referred to as data confined to a <a href="/wiki/Digital_data" title="Digital data">digital</a> environment and existence.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">Synthetic biology</a> is a new area of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> that combines science and <a href="/wiki/Biological_engineering" title="Biological engineering">biological engineering</a>. The common goal is the design and construction of new biological functions and systems not found in nature. Synthetic biology includes the broad redefinition and expansion of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, with the ultimate goals of being able to design and build engineered biological systems that process information, manipulate chemicals, fabricate materials and structures, produce energy, provide food, and maintain and enhance human health and the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">Death</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/220px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/330px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG/440px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animal corpses, like this <a href="/wiki/African_buffalo" title="African buffalo">African buffalo</a>, are recycled by the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>, providing energy and nutrients for living creatures</div></div></div>
<p>Death is the permanent termination of all vital functions or life processes in an organism or cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-define_death_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-define_death-211">[208]</a></sup> It can occur as a result of an accident, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_condition" title="Medical condition">medical conditions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biological_interaction" title="Biological interaction">biological interaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a>, or suicide. After death, the remains of an organism re-enter the <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycle</a>. Organisms may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necrophagy" title="Necrophagy">consumed</a> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predator</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenger</a> and leftover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic material</a> may then be further decomposed by <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>, organisms that recycle <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a>, returning it to the environment for reuse in the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.
</p><p>One of the challenges in defining death is in distinguishing it from life. Death would seem to refer to either the moment life ends, or when the state that follows life begins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-define_death_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-define_death-211">[208]</a></sup> However, determining when death has occurred is difficult, as cessation of life functions is often not simultaneous across organ systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[209]</a></sup> Such determination therefore requires drawing conceptual lines between life and death. This is problematic, however, because there is little consensus over how to define life. The nature of death has for millennia been a central concern of the world's religious traditions and of philosophical inquiry. Many religions maintain faith in either a kind of <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> of the body at a later date.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction">Extinction</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a></div>
<p>Extinction is the process by which a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">taxa</a> or <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> dies out, reducing biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup> The moment of extinction is generally considered the death of the last individual of that species. Because a species' potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">range</a> may be very large, determining this moment is difficult, and is usually done retrospectively after a period of apparent absence. Species become extinct when they are no longer able to survive in changing <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> or against superior competition. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" title="History of the Earth">Earth's history</a>, over 99% of all the species that have ever lived are extinct;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-107">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-109">[106]</a></sup> however, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a> may have accelerated evolution by providing opportunities for new groups of organisms to diversify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossils">Fossils</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">Fossils</a></div>
<p>Fossils are the preserved remains or <a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">traces</a> of animals, plants, and other organisms from the remote past. The totality of fossils, both discovered and undiscovered, and their placement in fossil-containing <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> formations and <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary</a> layers (<a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a>) is known as the <i>fossil record</i>. A preserved specimen is called a fossil if it is older than the arbitrary date of 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup> Hence, fossils range in age from the youngest at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> Epoch to the oldest from the <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archaean</a> Eon, up to 3.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The "evolution" of viruses and other similar forms is still uncertain. Therefore, this classification may be <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> because cellular life might have evolved from non-cellular life, or <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyletic</a> because the most recent common ancestor might not be included.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Infectious protein molecules <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">prions</a> are not considered living organisms, but can be described as "organism-comparable organic structures".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Certain specific organism-comparable organic structures may be considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subviral_agents" title="Subviral agents">subviral agents</a>, including virus-dependent entities: <a href="/wiki/Satellite_(biology)" title="Satellite (biology)">satellites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Defective_interfering_particle" title="Defective interfering particle">defective interfering particles</a>, both of which require <a href="/wiki/Helper_virus" title="Helper virus">another virus</a> for their replication.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
